reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoConfig

class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits

model_name = "facebook/bart-large"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name)
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 1023
batch_size = 1

def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

The self-attention for seq len 1023 is O(n^2) per layer. 12 layers => O(12 * n^2). The FFN is O(n * d * ffn_dim). Let's estimate compute. - d_model = 1024 - num_heads = 16 - head_dim = 64 - ffn_dim = 4096 For each layer: - Q, K, V projection: 3 * (1024 * 1024) = 3.1M params. For seq 1023, batch 1: flops ~ 2 * 3 * 1024 * 1024 * 1023 ≈ 6.4 GFLOPs. - Attention scores: batch*heads * seq * seq * head_dim = 1*16 * 1023 * 1023 * 64 ≈ 1.07 GFLOPs. - Attention output: 1*16 * 1023 * 1023 * 64 ≈ 1.07 GFLOPs. - Output projection: 1024 * 1024 * 1023 ≈ 1.07 GFLOPs. - FFN up: 1024 * 4096 * 1023 ≈ 4.3 GFLOPs. - FFN down: 4096 * 1024 * 1023 ≈ 4.3 GFLOPs. Total per layer: ~18 GFLOPs. 12 layers: ~216 GFLOPs. - Embeddings and LM head: embed ~1024*50265*1023 ≈ 52 GFLOPs (though embedding is just a lookup, not matmul). LM head: 1024*50265*1023 ≈ 52 GFLOPs. Total matmul FLOPs: ~270 GFLOPs. On an A100 (312 TFLOPS fp16, but we're fp32 so ~19.5 TFLOPS TF32 or ~156 TFLOPS fp32 sparse). If we can utilize tensor cores, ~270 GFLOPs / 100 TFLOPS = 2.7ms. But typical transformers are memory-bound for batch=1. The model weights are ~400M params => ~1.6GB in fp32. Reading them once per forward pass at 2TB/s takes ~0.8ms. But because of intermediate activations and overheads, actual time might be 30-50ms. So a large portion is overhead. The `transformers` attention computes: ``` attn_weights = torch.matmul(query_states, key_states.transpose(-1, -2)) * scale attn_weights = attn_weights + attention_mask attn_weights = nn.functional.softmax(attn_weights, dim=-1) attn_weights = nn.functional.dropout(attn_weights, p=dropout, training=False) attn_output = torch.matmul(attn_weights, value_states) ``` Then a linear projection. That's essentially what we need. Let's explore how much code that would be. We can write a generic C++ function that does BART decoder forward. We can simplify by writing it as a series of `at::` calls. For example: ```cpp torch::Tensor forward(torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor embed_tokens_weight, torch::Tensor embed_positions_weight, // ... many other weights ... ) { auto x = at::embedding(embed_tokens_weight, input_ids); auto positions = ...; x = x + at::embedding(embed_positions_weight, positions); x = at::dropout(x, 0.0, false); for (int layer = 0; layer < 12; ++layer) { // self_attn auto residual = x; x = at::layer_norm(x, {1024}, ln_weight[layer], ln_bias[layer], 1e-5); auto q = at::linear(x, q_weight[layer], q_bias[layer]); auto k = at::linear(x, k_weight[layer], k_bias[layer]); auto v = at::linear(x, v_weight[layer], v_bias[layer]); q = q.view({batch, seq, 16, 64}).transpose(1,2); k = k.view({batch, seq, 16, 64}).transpose(1,2); v = v.view({batch, seq, 16, 64}).transpose(1,2); auto attn = at::matmul(q, k.transpose(-2,-1)) * scale; attn = attn + causal_mask; attn = at::softmax(attn, -1); attn = at::dropout(attn, 0.0, false); auto out = at::matmul(attn, v); out = out.transpose(1,2).contiguous().view({batch, seq, 1024}); out = at::linear(out, out_proj_weight[layer], out_proj_bias[layer]); x = residual + at::dropout(out, 0.0, false); // FFN residual = x; x = at::layer_norm(x, {1024}, ln2_weight[layer], ln2_bias[layer], 1e-5); auto ffn = at::linear(x, fc1_weight[layer], fc1_bias[layer]); ffn = at::gelu(ffn); ffn = at::linear(ffn, fc2_weight[layer], fc2_bias[layer]); x = residual + at::dropout(ffn, 0.0, false); } x = at::layer_norm(x, {1024}, final_ln_weight, final_ln_bias, 1e-5); x = at::linear(x, lm_head_weight, lm_head_bias); return x; } ``` This is a lot of parameters to pass (many tensors). But we can pack them into a `std::vector<torch::Tensor>` or a struct. In Python, we extract them. This C++ code is manageable. It uses `at::` functions which are already highly optimized. Actually, `BartDecoderLayer` structure: ```python class BartDecoderLayer(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__() self.embed_dim = config.d_model self.self_attn = BartAttention(...) self.dropout = config.dropout self.activation_fn = ACT2FN[config.activation_function] # gelu self.activation_dropout = config.activation_dropout self.self_attn_layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(self.embed_dim) self.fc1 = nn.Linear(self.embed_dim, config.decoder_ffn_dim) self.fc2 = nn.Linear(config.decoder_ffn_dim, self.embed_dim) self.final_layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(self.embed_dim) def forward(self, hidden_states, ...): residual = hidden_states hidden_states = self.self_attn_layer_norm(hidden_states) hidden_states, attn_weights, present_key_value = self.self_attn(hidden_states=hidden_states, ...) hidden_states = nn.functional.dropout(hidden_states, p=self.dropout, training=self.training) hidden_states = residual + hidden_states residual = hidden_states hidden_states = self.final_layer_norm(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.activation_fn(self.fc1(hidden_states)) hidden_states = nn.functional.dropout(hidden_states, p=self.activation_dropout, training=self.training) hidden_states = self.fc2(hidden_states) hidden_states = nn.functional.dropout(hidden_states, p=self.dropout, training=self.training) hidden_states = residual + hidden_states return hidden_states ``` Wait, there might be slight differences. Let's check exact order: In `BartDecoderLayer`: ``` residual = hidden_states hidden_states = self.self_attn_layer_norm(hidden_states) # Self Attention hidden_states, attn_weights, present_key_value = self.self_attn( hidden_states=hidden_states, attention_mask=attention_mask, layer_head_mask=layer_head_mask, past_key_value=past_key_value, output_attentions=output_attentions, ) hidden_states = nn.functional.dropout(hidden_states, p=self.dropout, training=self.training) hidden_states = residual + hidden_states # Fully Connected residual = hidden_states hidden_states = self.final_layer_norm(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.activation_fn(self.fc1(hidden_states)) hidden_states = nn.functional.dropout(hidden_states, p=self.activation_dropout, training=self.training) hidden_states = self.fc2(hidden_states) hidden_states = nn.functional.dropout(hidden_states, p=self.dropout, training=self.training) hidden_states = residual + hidden_states return hidden_states ``` Yes, that's it. `BartAttention` forward: ```python class BartAttention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, embed_dim, num_heads, dropout=0.0, is_decoder=False, bias=True): super().__init__() self.embed_dim = embed_dim self.num_heads = num_heads self.dropout = dropout self.head_dim = embed_dim // num_heads self.scaling = self.head_dim ** -0.5 self.k_proj = nn.Linear(embed_dim, embed_dim, bias=bias) self.v_proj = nn.Linear(embed_dim, embed_dim, bias=bias) self.q_proj = nn.Linear(embed_dim, embed_dim, bias=bias) self.out_proj = nn.Linear(embed_dim, embed_dim, bias=bias) def forward(self, hidden_states, key_value_states=None, past_key_value=None, attention_mask=None, layer_head_mask=None, output_attentions=False): bsz, tgt_len, _ = hidden_states.size() query_states = self.q_proj(hidden_states) * self.scaling if key_value_states is None: key_states = self._shape(self.k_proj(hidden_states), -1, bsz) value_states = self._shape(self.v_proj(hidden_states), -1, bsz) else: ... proj_shape = (bsz * self.num_heads, -1, self.head_dim) query_states = self._shape(query_states, tgt_len, bsz) query_states = query_states.view(*proj_shape) key_states = key_states.view(*proj_shape) value_states = value_states.view(*proj_shape) src_len = key_states.size(1) attn_weights = torch.bmm(query_states, key_states.transpose(1, 2)) if attention_mask is not None: attn_weights += attention_mask attn_weights = nn.functional.softmax(attn_weights, dim=-1) if layer_head_mask is not None: ... attn_weights = nn.functional.dropout(attn_weights, p=self.dropout, training=self.training) attn_output = torch.bmm(attn_weights, value_states) attn_output = attn_output.view(bsz, self.num_heads, tgt_len, self.head_dim) attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1, 2) attn_output = attn_output.reshape(bsz, tgt_len, self.embed_dim) attn_output = self.out_proj(attn_output) return attn_output, attn_weights, past_key_value def _shape(self, tensor, seq_len, bsz): return tensor.view(bsz, seq_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2).contiguous() ``` Wait, `_shape` returns a view: `tensor.view(bsz, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim).transpose(1,2).contiguous()`. So after `_shape`, it's `(bsz, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim)`. The scaling is applied to `query_states` before reshaping. In `forward`, `query_states = self.q_proj(hidden_states) * self.scaling`. Then `query_states = self._shape(query_states, tgt_len, bsz)`. This returns contiguous tensor of shape `(bsz, num_heads, tgt_len, head_dim)`. Then `proj_shape = (bsz * num_heads, -1, head_dim)`. `query_states = query_states.view(*proj_shape)`. Same for key and value. `attn_weights = torch.bmm(query_states, key_states.transpose(1, 2))`. `query_states` is `(bsz*num_heads, tgt_len, head_dim)`. `key_states.transpose(1,2)` is `(bsz*num_heads, head_dim, src_len)`. So `attn_weights` is `(bsz*num_heads, tgt_len, src_len)`. If `attention_mask` is not None, it's added. The mask shape is typically `(bsz, 1, tgt_len, src_len)` or `(1, 1, tgt_len, src_len)`, which broadcasts to `(bsz*num_heads, tgt_len, src_len)`. `softmax` over dim=-1. Then `dropout`. `attn_output = torch.bmm(attn_weights, value_states)` where `value_states` is `(bsz*num_heads, src_len, head_dim)`. Output shape: `(bsz*num_heads, tgt_len, head_dim)`. `attn_output = attn_output.view(bsz, num_heads, tgt_len, head_dim)`. `transpose(1,2)` -> `(bsz, tgt_len, num_heads, head_dim)`. `reshape(bsz, tgt_len, embed_dim)`. Then `out_proj`. This is the exact flow. We need to replicate numerically. If we use `at::bmm` or `at::matmul` in C++ with the same shapes, we should get identical results (cuBLAS deterministic at same algorithm). However, the `view` and `transpose` might produce non-contiguous tensors. `at::bmm` requires contiguous? Actually, `torch.bmm` handles strides, and may copy to contiguous internally. In C++, `at::bmm` is the same. The attention mask addition: if mask is `-inf` for upper triangle, adding to attn_weights yields `nan` for positions where both attn_weights and mask are `-inf`? Actually, mask is 0 on lower triangle, `-inf` on upper. If `attention_mask` is `-inf` for the upper triangle, adding to attn_weights (which are finite) gives `-inf`. The softmax of `-inf` is 0. But if some entries of `attn_weights` are also `-inf` from previous operations (unlikely), then `-inf + -inf = -inf` (or nan in some implementations?). In PyTorch, `-inf + -inf = -inf`, so it's fine. But we need to ensure we use the same mask values. In `transformers`, the causal mask is constructed as a float tensor of min value. For fp32, it's `torch.finfo(torch.float32).min` which is `-3.4028234663852886e+38`. In C++, we can use `-std::numeric_limits<float>::infinity()`? Actually, PyTorch's `torch.finfo(float).min` is the smallest representable float, `-float_max`, not `-inf`. Let's verify: In Python, `torch.finfo(torch.float32).min` is `-3.4028234663852886e+38`. So if we use `-INFINITY` in CUDA, it might be slightly different? But adding `-inf` to a finite number yields `-inf`, and softmax of `-inf` is 0. Using `-float_max` vs `-inf` might behave the same for softmax. However, to be safe, we should use the same value. But generating the mask in C++ with `-std::numeric_limits<float>::max() * -1.0f` gives `-3.4028235e+38`, which matches. Alternatively, we can pass the precomputed causal mask from Python to the C++ function. That would be easier and avoid any mismatch in mask construction. We can generate it once in `__init__` using the same code as the model (or just using the model's buffer if it has one). Or we can store the mask as a constant in C++. Another subtlety: `LayerNorm` in PyTorch uses `eps`. BART config default `layer_norm_epsilon` is `1e-5`. The `nn.LayerNorm` module stores `weight` and `bias`. We need to use `at::layer_norm` with the same parameters. `GELU`: `transformers` uses `torch.nn.functional.gelu` (exact). `at::gelu` in C++ corresponds to exact GELU (with `'none'` approximation). Actually, in PyTorch C++, `at::gelu` has a `approximate` parameter? In older versions, it might just be exact. We need to be careful. `transformers` Bart uses `ACT2FN["gelu"]` which is `GELUActivation()`. In `transformers`, `GELUActivation` calls `torch.nn.functional.gelu(input, approximate="none")` (since PyTorch 1.12?). Actually, `transformers` defines `class GELUActivation(nn.Module): def forward(self, input): return nn.functional.gelu(input, approximate="none")`. So exact GELU. `at::gelu` should match exactly if it's the exact version. Let's check: In PyTorch C++ API, `at::gelu(const Tensor& self)` calls the exact implementation. There is also `at::gelu(const Tensor& self, c10::string_view approximate)`. So `at::gelu(x)` is exact. Good. `Dropout`: in eval mode, dropout is identity (no-op). We can just skip it if we set `training=false`. Or use `at::dropout(x, p, false)`. `embed_positions`: We need to replicate `BartLearnedPositionalEmbedding`. The weight tensor is `embed_positions.weight`. In `BartDecoder`, `self.embed_positions` is an instance of `BartLearnedPositionalEmbedding(offset=2)`. It takes `input_ids` and returns embeddings for positions `torch.arange(seq_len, device=input_ids.device) + self.offset`. So we can compute `positions = at::arange(seq_len, input_ids.options().dtype(torch::kLong)) + 2; pos_embeds = at::embedding(embed_positions_weight, positions);`. `embed_tokens`: `at::embedding(embed_tokens_weight, input_ids)`. Note: `nn.Embedding` has `padding_idx=1` for BART. `at::embedding` with `padding_idx=1` will zero out row 1. The default `at::embedding` might not have `padding_idx`. But in C++, there is `at::embedding(const Tensor& weight, const Tensor& indices, int64_t padding_idx=-1, bool scale_grad_by_freq=false, bool sparse=false)`. So we can pass `1` as padding_idx. Then dropout. Now, for the attention mask: In `BartForCausalLM.forward`, if no `attention_mask` is provided, it creates one. Actually, `BartDecoder.forward` calls `self._prepare_decoder_attention_mask(attention_mask, input_shape, inputs_embeds, past_key_values_length)`. If `attention_mask` is None, it creates a causal mask. The mask shape: `(batch_size, 1, tgt_len, src_len)`. For causal, it uses `_make_causal_mask(input_shape, inputs_embeds.dtype, device, past_key_values_length)`. The min value is `torch.finfo(dtype).min`. We can generate it in C++: ```cpp auto causal_mask = at::full({seq_len, seq_len}, -std::numeric_limits<float>::max(), hidden_states.options()); auto mask_cond = at::arange(seq_len, hidden_states.options().dtype(torch::kLong)); auto mask_cond_col = (mask_cond + 1).view({seq_len, 1}); auto mask_cond_row = mask_cond.view({1, seq_len}); causal_mask = at::where(mask_cond_row < mask_cond_col, causal_mask, at::zeros({1}, hidden_states.options())); ``` Wait, we want 0 on lower triangle (including diagonal), and min on upper triangle. Condition for upper triangle: `col > row` i.e., `j > i`. Or equivalently, `row < col`. Using `mask_cond_row < mask_cond_col`: For i=0,j=0: 0 < 1 -> False -> 0 For i=0,j=1: 0 < 2 -> True -> min Wait, `(mask_cond + 1).view({seq_len, 1})` is a column vector: element i is i+1. `mask_cond.view({1, seq_len})` is a row vector: element j is j. Condition `j < i+1` -> `j <= i`. So lower triangle (including diagonal) is True -> we want those to be 0. Wait, `at::where(condition, x, y)` returns x where condition is True, else y. If condition is `j < i+1` (i.e., `j <= i`), that's the lower triangle. We want lower triangle to be 0. So: `causal_mask = at::where(mask_cond_row < mask_cond_col, at::full(..., min), at::zeros(...))`. Wait, `mask_cond_row < mask_cond_col` is `j < i+1`? Let's be careful. `mask_cond_col` = column vector of shape (seq_len, 1) with values `(mask_cond + 1)`. `mask_cond_row` = row vector of shape (1, seq_len) with values `mask_cond`. broadcasting: condition shape (seq_len, seq_len). element (i,j): `mask_cond_row[0,j] < mask_cond_col[i,0]` => `j < i+1`. This is true for `j <= i` (lower triangle including diagonal). element (i,j) where `j > i`: false (upper triangle). So `at::where(condition, true_val, false_val)`: lower triangle gets `true_val`, upper triangle gets `false_val`. We want lower triangle = 0, upper triangle = min. So: `causal_mask = at::where(mask_cond_row < mask_cond_col, at::zeros({1}, opts), at::full({1}, min, opts));` But in PyTorch transformers, they do: ```python mask = torch.full((tgt_len, tgt_len), torch.finfo(dtype).min) mask_cond = torch.arange(mask.size(-1)) mask.masked_fill_(mask_cond < (mask_cond + 1).view(mask.size(-1), 1), 0) ``` This uses `masked_fill_(condition, 0)`. The condition is `mask_cond < (mask_cond + 1).view(..., 1)`. `mask_cond` is a 1D tensor of shape (tgt_len,). `(mask_cond + 1).view(tgt_len, 1)` is column vector. Broadcasting: condition[i,j] = `j < i+1` => `j <= i`. So lower triangle is True. `masked_fill_(True, 0)` sets lower triangle to 0. Upper triangle remains min. So the result is lower triangle = 0, upper triangle = min. So our `where` should be: `causal_mask = at::where(mask_cond_row < mask_cond_col, at::zeros({1}, opts), at::full({1}, min, opts));` Yes. Then we need to expand to shape `(batch, 1, seq_len, seq_len)`. `causal_mask = causal_mask.unsqueeze(0).unsqueeze(0);` In `transformers`, there might be an additional `attention_mask` if `input_ids` contains padding. But our inputs are random integers, which may include padding token (1). However, `BartDecoder` doesn't use `attention_mask` for causal LM if not provided; it only generates the causal mask. Let's look up `transformers.models.bart.modeling_bart.BartForCausalLM.forward`. ```python def forward( self, input_ids: torch.LongTensor = None, attention_mask: Optional[torch.Tensor] = None, ... ): ... if attention_mask is None: attention_mask = input_ids.new_ones(input_ids.shape, dtype=torch.long) ... outputs = self.model.decoder( input_ids=input_ids, attention_mask=attention_mask, ... ) logits = self.lm_head(outputs[0]) return CausalLMOutputWithCrossAttentions(logits=logits, ...) ``` Then inside `BartDecoder.forward`, it calls `self._prepare_decoder_attention_mask(attention_mask, input_shape, inputs_embeds, past_key_values_length)`. `_prepare_decoder_attention_mask` does: ```python combined_attention_mask = _make_causal_mask(...) if attention_mask is not None: expanded_attn_mask = _expand_mask(attention_mask, inputs_embeds.dtype, tgt_len=input_shape[-1]).to(inputs_embeds.device) combined_attention_mask = expanded_attn_mask + combined_attention_mask return combined_attention_mask ``` Where `_expand_mask` converts a `(batch, seq_len)` mask of 1s/0s to a `(batch, 1, tgt_len, src_len)` mask of 0s/min. Specifically, it does: ```python def _expand_mask(mask, dtype, tgt_len=None): bsz, src_len = mask.size() tgt_len = tgt_len if tgt_len is not None else src_len expanded_mask = mask[:, None, None, :].expand(bsz, 1, tgt_len, src_len).to(dtype) inverted_mask = 1.0 - expanded_mask return inverted_mask.masked_fill(inverted_mask.to(torch.bool), torch.finfo(dtype).min) ``` So if `attention_mask` is all ones, `inverted_mask` is all zeros, and `masked_fill` does nothing. So `expanded_attn_mask` is all zeros. Thus the combined mask is just the causal mask. So if our input contains padding tokens (1), the `attention_mask` generated by `BartForCausalLM` would have zeros at padding positions, which would affect the mask. Let's see how many lines it would be. C++ code outline: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <vector> torch::Tensor bart_forward(torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor attention_mask, std::vector<torch::Tensor> w) { int idx = 0; auto embed_tokens_weight = w[idx++]; auto embed_positions_weight = w[idx++]; // ... for each layer: 10 tensors // final layernorm // lm_head_weight int batch = input_ids.size(0); int seq_len = input_ids.size(1); auto opts = input_ids.options().dtype(torch::kFloat32); // maybe use float32 for everything // embeddings auto inputs_embeds = at::embedding(embed_tokens_weight, input_ids, 1); auto positions = at::arange(2, seq_len + 2, opts.dtype(torch::kLong)); auto pos_embeds = at::embedding(embed_positions_weight, positions); auto hidden_states = inputs_embeds + pos_embeds.unsqueeze(0); // hidden_states = at::dropout(hidden_states, 0.0, false); // skip if eval for (int layer = 0; layer < 12; ++layer) { auto residual = hidden_states; auto sa_ln_w = w[idx++]; auto sa_ln_b = w[idx++]; hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {1024}, sa_ln_w, sa_ln_b, 1e-5); auto q_w = w[idx++], q_b = w[idx++]; auto k_w = w[idx++], k_b = w[idx++]; auto v_w = w[idx++], v_b = w[idx++]; auto out_w = w[idx++], out_b = w[idx++]; auto q = at::linear(hidden_states, q_w, q_b) * 0.125; // head_dim=64, scaling=1/sqrt(64)=0.125 auto k = at::linear(hidden_states, k_w, k_b); auto v = at::linear(hidden_states, v_w, v_b); q = q.view({batch, seq_len, 16, 64}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous().view({batch * 16, seq_len, 64}); k = k.view({batch, seq_len, 16, 64}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous().view({batch * 16, seq_len, 64}); v = v.view({batch, seq_len, 16, 64}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous().view({batch * 16, seq_len, 64}); auto attn_weights = at::bmm(q, k.transpose(1, 2)); attn_weights = attn_weights + attention_mask; // attention_mask is (batch, 1, seq, seq) broadcasted attn_weights = at::softmax(attn_weights, -1); // dropout skipped auto attn_output = at::bmm(attn_weights, v); attn_output = attn_output.view({batch, 16, seq_len, 64}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous().view({batch, seq_len, 1024}); attn_output = at::linear(attn_output, out_w, out_b); hidden_states = residual + attn_output; residual = hidden_states; auto ffn_ln_w = w[idx++], ffn_ln_b = w[idx++]; hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {1024}, ffn_ln_w, ffn_ln_b, 1e-5); auto fc1_w = w[idx++], fc1_b = w[idx++]; auto fc2_w = w[idx++], fc2_b = w[idx++]; hidden_states = at::linear(hidden_states, fc1_w, fc1_b); hidden_states = at::gelu(hidden_states); hidden_states = at::linear(hidden_states, fc2_w, fc2_b); hidden_states = residual + hidden_states; } auto final_ln_w = w[idx++], final_ln_b = w[idx++]; hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {1024}, final_ln_w, final_ln_b, 1e-5); auto lm_head_w = w[idx++]; auto logits = at::linear(hidden_states, lm_head_w, {}); return logits; } ``` Wait, we need to count the weights carefully. In PyTorch, `nn.Linear` stores weight as `(out_features, in_features)`. `at::linear` uses this convention. So we pass the weight tensor directly. `at::bmm` signature: ```cpp Tensor bmm(const Tensor& self, const Tensor& mat2); ``` One subtlety: `transformers` uses `torch.bmm` or `torch.matmul` for attention. We can use `at::matmul` which handles broadcasting and returns the same as `torch.matmul`. Actually, `at::matmul` might be easier because `q` and `k.t()` are 3D tensors. `at::matmul(q, k.transpose(1,2))` works. But `at::bmm` might require contiguous inputs. To be safe, use `at::matmul`. Let's verify: `at::matmul(q, k.transpose(1,2))` where q is `(BS*H, T, D)` and k.t() is `(BS*H, D, T)`. The result is `(BS*H, T, T)`. In PyTorch, `torch.matmul` does exactly this. `torch.bmm` also works. Using `at::bmm` after making contiguous should be fine. But `at::matmul` is more general and handles strides. Let's use `at::matmul`. Another subtlety: in `transformers`, `attn_weights += attention_mask`. The `attention_mask` is typically `(batch, 1, tgt_len, src_len)` broadcasted over heads. Since we reshaped query etc. to `(batch*heads, tgt, src)`, and `attention_mask` is `(batch, 1, tgt, src)`, broadcasting should work when we add. In PyTorch, `attn_weights.view(batch, heads, tgt, src) + attention_mask` then view back. But in `transformers`, they do `attn_weights += attention_mask` directly after `bmm`. Because `attn_weights` is `(batch*heads, tgt, src)` and `attention_mask` is `(batch, 1, tgt, src)`. PyTorch broadcasts this automatically? Let's check: shape `(B*H, T, S)` + `(B, 1, T, S)`? Wait, `(B*H, T, S)` is 3D, `(B, 1, T, S)` is 4D. Broadcasting rules: prepend dims of size 1 to the smaller-rank tensor until ranks match. So `(1, B*H, T, S)` vs `(B, 1, T, S)`. For these to broadcast, `1` must match `B` and `B*H` must match `1`, which fails unless B=1 and H=1. But in `transformers`, they might reshape the mask to `(batch*heads, 1, tgt_len, src_len)`? No, they don't. Wait, in `transformers`, `attention_mask` passed to `BartAttention` is `combined_attention_mask` of shape `(batch_size, 1, tgt_len, src_len)`. Otherwise broadcasting fails (on CPU) or does something weird (on GPU might silently broadcast incorrectly? Actually, PyTorch would error). Another thing: `attention_mask` may be a 4D tensor. One more subtlety: `at::softmax` along dim=-1 on a tensor with `-inf` values yields 0 for those positions (as long as not all are -inf). In causal attention, each row has some finite values and some -inf, so softmax works. Now, let's consider dropout. In training, dropout is applied to attention weights and hidden states. In eval, `training=False`. The evaluator likely does inference. We can just skip dropout (i.e., pass `training=false` to `at::dropout` which returns the input unchanged). But if the evaluator somehow runs with `training=True` (unlikely), our output would differ because we skip dropout. But dropout is random, making correctness impossible to match unless we set the same seed. Since the evaluator checks numerical equivalence, it must run in eval mode or with dropout disabled. So we can assume eval mode. To be safe, we can just not call dropout. Now, let's think about the `padding_idx` for embedding. `BartDecoder.embed_tokens` has `padding_idx=1`. Then `positions = positions + self.offset` where `offset = 2`. So for `seq_len=1023`, positions are `2..1024`. `embed_positions.weight` shape is `(1026, 1024)` (if `max_pos=1024` and `offset=2`). So indices up to 1024 are valid. Good. Now, what about `attention_mask` generation in Python? We can generate it exactly as the reference does. In the reference, `BartForCausalLM.forward` creates `attention_mask` if None. For `BartDecoder`, it then builds `combined_attention_mask`. But since we are doing the whole forward in C++, we could pass the 4D `combined_attention_mask` from Python. We can compute it in Python like this: ```python def _make_causal_mask(seq_len, dtype, device): mask = torch.full((seq_len, seq_len), torch.finfo(dtype).min, device=device, dtype=dtype) mask_cond = torch.arange(mask.size(-1), device=device) mask.masked_fill_(mask_cond < (mask_cond + 1).view(mask.size(-1), 1), 0) return mask def _expand_mask(mask, dtype, tgt_len): bsz, src_len = mask.size() expanded_mask = mask[:, None, None, :].expand(bsz, 1, tgt_len, src_len).to(dtype) inverted_mask = 1.0 - expanded_mask return inverted_mask.masked_fill(inverted_mask.to(torch.bool), torch.finfo(dtype).min) # In forward: seq_len = x.shape[1] attention_mask = (x != self.config.pad_token_id).to(self.dtype).to(x.device) expanded_mask = _expand_mask(attention_mask, self.dtype, seq_len) causal_mask = _make_causal_mask(seq_len, self.dtype, x.device).unsqueeze(0).unsqueeze(0) combined_mask = expanded_mask + causal_mask ``` Wait, the causal mask should be broadcasted across batch. In `_make_causal_mask`, the output is `(1, 1, seq_len, seq_len)` after unsqueezing. In PyTorch's `_prepare_decoder_attention_mask` for BART, the causal mask is `(bsz, 1, tgt_len, tgt_len)`? Actually, `_make_causal_mask` returns `(bsz, 1, tgt_len, tgt_len)` because it takes `input_ids_shape`. But since the causal mask is the same for all batch items, broadcasting works. In PyTorch, it's `(1, 1, tgt_len, tgt_len)`. When adding to `expanded_mask` of shape `(bsz, 1, tgt_len, src_len)`, broadcasting aligns. So we can create it as `(1, 1, seq_len, seq_len)`. Then pass `combined_mask` to C++. But wait, `expanded_mask` is 0 for real tokens, min for pad tokens. `causal_mask` is 0 for lower triangle, min for upper. Adding them: - Lower triangle, real token: 0 + 0 = 0 - Lower triangle, pad token: min + 0 = min - Upper triangle, real token: 0 + min = min - Upper triangle, pad token: min + min = -inf (or min*2) All correct. Let's draft it carefully. ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <vector> #include <cmath> torch::Tensor bart_forward(torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor attention_mask, std::vector<torch::Tensor> w) { int idx = 0; auto embed_tokens_weight = w[idx++]; auto embed_positions_weight = w[idx++]; auto layernorm_embedding_weight = w[idx++]; auto layernorm_embedding_bias = w[idx++]; int batch = input_ids.size(0); int seq_len = input_ids.size(1); int d_model = 1024; int num_heads = 16; int head_dim = 64; int num_layers = 12; float scale = std::sqrt((float)head_dim); scale = 1.0f / scale; auto opts = input_ids.options().dtype(torch::kFloat32); // Embeddings auto inputs_embeds = at::embedding(embed_tokens_weight, input_ids, 1); auto positions = at::arange(2, seq_len + 2, opts.dtype(torch::kLong)); auto pos_embeds = at::embedding(embed_positions_weight, positions); auto hidden_states = inputs_embeds + pos_embeds.unsqueeze(0); hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {d_model}, layernorm_embedding_weight, layernorm_embedding_bias, 1e-5); // hidden_states = at::dropout(hidden_states, 0.0, false); // no-op for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; ++layer) { auto residual = hidden_states; auto sa_ln_w = w[idx++]; auto sa_ln_b = w[idx++]; hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {d_model}, sa_ln_w, sa_ln_b, 1e-5); auto k = at::linear(hidden_states, k_w, k_b); auto v = at::linear(hidden_states, v_w, v_b); q = q.view({batch, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous().view({batch * num_heads, seq_len, head_dim}); k = k.view({batch, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous().view({batch * num_heads, seq_len, head_dim}); v = v.view({batch, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous().view({batch * num_heads, seq_len, head_dim}); attn_output = at::linear(attn_output, out_w, out_b); hidden_states = residual + attn_output; residual = hidden_states; auto ffn_ln_w = w[idx++]; auto ffn_ln_b = w[idx++]; hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {d_model}, ffn_ln_w, ffn_ln_b, 1e-5); The weights are initially on CPU after `from_pretrained` unless `device_map` is used. We can keep them on CPU and move in forward when we see the input device. However, moving 200 tensors from CPU to GPU on each forward is slow. We should move them to GPU once and cache them. We can do it on the first forward call: ```python def _prepare_weights(self, device): if self._device != device: self._weights_on_device = [w.to(device) for w in self.weights] self._device = device ``` But some weights might be on meta device? No, we loaded them normally. Now, let's think about the `functions` argument to `load_inline`. We need to list the names of the C++ functions. We have `bart_forward`. Let's draft the full C++ source code. It will be a string in Python. ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <vector> #include <cmath> torch::Tensor bart_forward(torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor attention_mask, std::vector<torch::Tensor> w, int has_layernorm_embedding, int has_final_layer_norm) { int idx = 0; auto embed_tokens_weight = w[idx++]; auto embed_positions_weight = w[idx++]; torch::Tensor layernorm_embedding_weight, layernorm_embedding_bias; if (has_layernorm_embedding) { layernorm_embedding_weight = w[idx++]; layernorm_embedding_bias = w[idx++]; } else { idx += 2; } int batch = input_ids.size(0); int seq_len = input_ids.size(1); int d_model = 1024; int num_heads = 16; int head_dim = 64; int num_layers = 12; float scale = 1.0f / std::sqrt((float)head_dim); auto opts = input_ids.options().dtype(torch::kFloat32); auto inputs_embeds = at::embedding(embed_tokens_weight, input_ids, 1); auto positions = at::arange(2, seq_len + 2, opts.dtype(torch::kLong)); auto pos_embeds = at::embedding(embed_positions_weight, positions); auto hidden_states = inputs_embeds + pos_embeds.unsqueeze(0); if (has_layernorm_embedding) { hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {d_model}, layernorm_embedding_weight, layernorm_embedding_bias, 1e-5); } for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; ++layer) { auto residual = hidden_states; auto sa_ln_w = w[idx++]; auto sa_ln_b = w[idx++]; hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {d_model}, sa_ln_w, sa_ln_b, 1e-5); auto q_w = w[idx++]; auto q_b = w[idx++]; auto k_w = w[idx++]; auto k_b = w[idx++]; auto v_w = w[idx++]; auto v_b = w[idx++]; auto out_w = w[idx++]; auto out_b = w[idx++]; auto q = at::linear(hidden_states, q_w, q_b) * scale; auto k = at::linear(hidden_states, k_w, k_b); auto v = at::linear(hidden_states, v_w, v_b); q = q.view({batch, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous().view({batch * num_heads, seq_len, head_dim}); k = k.view({batch, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous().view({batch * num_heads, seq_len, head_dim}); v = v.view({batch, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous().view({batch * num_heads, seq_len, head_dim}); auto attn_weights = at::bmm(q, k.transpose(1, 2)); attn_weights = attn_weights.view({batch, num_heads, seq_len, seq_len}); attn_weights = attn_weights + attention_mask; attn_weights = attn_weights.view({batch * num_heads, seq_len, seq_len}); attn_weights = at::softmax(attn_weights, -1); auto attn_output = at::bmm(attn_weights, v); attn_output = attn_output.view({batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous().view({batch, seq_len, d_model}); attn_output = at::linear(attn_output, out_w, out_b); hidden_states = residual + attn_output; residual = hidden_states; auto ffn_ln_w = w[idx++]; auto ffn_ln_b = w[idx++]; hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {d_model}, ffn_ln_w, ffn_ln_b, 1e-5); auto fc1_w = w[idx++]; auto fc1_b = w[idx++]; auto fc2_w = w[idx++]; auto fc2_b = w[idx++]; hidden_states = at::linear(hidden_states, fc1_w, fc1_b); hidden_states = at::gelu(hidden_states); hidden_states = at::linear(hidden_states, fc2_w, fc2_b); hidden_states = residual + hidden_states; } if (has_final_layer_norm) { auto final_ln_w = w[idx++]; auto final_ln_b = w[idx++]; hidden_states = at::layer_norm(hidden_states, {d_model}, final_ln_w, final_ln_b, 1e-5); } else { idx += 2; } auto lm_head_w = w[idx++]; auto logits = at::linear(hidden_states, lm_head_w, {}); return logits; } ``` Wait, we need to verify the order of weights per layer. In our Python code, we appended: - self_attn_layer_norm.weight - self_attn_layer_norm.bias - q_proj.weight, q_proj.bias - k_proj.weight, k_proj.bias - v_proj.weight, v_proj.bias - out_proj.weight, out_proj.bias - final_layer_norm.weight, final_layer_norm.bias - fc1.weight, fc1.bias - fc2.weight, fc2.bias But in the C++ code, after `sa_ln_w/b`, we do q_w, q_b, k_w, k_b, v_w, v_b, out_w, out_b, then after attention `ffn_ln_w/b`, then `fc1_w/b`, `fc2_w/b`. This matches the order of appends. Good. But we also need to ensure `lm_head_w` is extracted correctly. In `BartForCausalLM`, `lm_head.weight` shares storage with `embed_tokens.weight`. When we extracted `decoder.embed_tokens.weight` earlier, and now `self.model.lm_head.weight`, they might be the same tensor object (same Python id). That's fine. Passing the same tensor twice in the vector is fine. Now, we need to consider if `self.model.lm_head` has a bias. Let's write the `forward` method: ```python def forward(self, x): device = x.device dtype = self.dtype # float32 if self._device != device: self._weights = [w.to(device) for w in self.weights] self._device = device batch, seq_len = x.shape # Build attention mask attention_mask_2d = (x != self.config.pad_token_id).to(dtype) inverted_mask = 1.0 - attention_mask_2d.unsqueeze(1).unsqueeze(2) min_val = torch.finfo(dtype).min expanded_attn_mask = inverted_mask.masked_fill(inverted_mask.to(torch.bool), min_val) # causal mask causal_mask = torch.full((seq_len, seq_len), min_val, device=device, dtype=dtype) mask_cond = torch.arange(seq_len, device=device) causal_mask.masked_fill_(mask_cond < (mask_cond + 1).view(seq_len, 1), 0) causal_mask = causal_mask.unsqueeze(0).unsqueeze(0) combined_mask = expanded_attn_mask + causal_mask # call C++ extension logits = self.cpp_module.bart_forward(x, combined_mask, self._weights, int(self.has_layernorm_embedding), int(self.has_final_layer_norm)) return logits ``` Wait, `_expand_mask` in transformers for `causal LM` actually does: ```python def _expand_mask(mask, dtype, tgt_len=None): bsz, src_len = mask.size() tgt_len = tgt_len if tgt_len is not None else src_len expanded_mask = mask[:, None, None, :].expand(bsz, 1, tgt_len, src_len).to(dtype) inverted_mask = 1.0 - expanded_mask return inverted_mask.masked_fill(inverted_mask.to(torch.bool), torch.finfo(dtype).min) ``` In our case, `mask` is `(batch, seq_len)`. `tgt_len = seq_len`. `expanded_mask` is `(batch, 1, seq_len, seq_len)`. `inverted_mask` is 0 for real tokens, 1 for pad. `masked_fill_(bool, min)` sets pad positions to min. So `expanded_attn_mask` is 0 for real, min for pad. That's what we did. But let's verify the exact function in `transformers`. In `BartDecoder._prepare_decoder_attention_mask`: ```python def _prepare_decoder_attention_mask(self, attention_mask, input_shape, inputs_embeds, past_key_values_length): # create causal mask combined_attention_mask = _make_causal_mask( input_shape, inputs_embeds.dtype, device=inputs_embeds.device, past_key_values_length=past_key_values_length, ) if attention_mask is not None: # [bsz, seq_len] -> [bsz, 1, tgt_seq_len, src_seq_len] expanded_attn_mask = _expand_mask(attention_mask, inputs_embeds.dtype, tgt_len=input_shape[-1]).to(inputs_embeds.device) combined_attention_mask = ( expanded_attn_mask if combined_attention_mask is None else expanded_attn_mask + combined_attention_mask ) return combined_attention_mask ``` And `_make_causal_mask`: ```python def _make_causal_mask(input_ids_shape, dtype, device, past_key_values_length=0): bsz, tgt_len = input_ids_shape mask = torch.full((tgt_len, tgt_len), torch.finfo(dtype).min, device=device) mask_cond = torch.arange(mask.size(-1), device=device) mask.masked_fill_(mask_cond < (mask_cond + 1).view(mask.size(-1), 1), 0) mask = mask.to(dtype) if past_key_values_length > 0: mask = torch.cat([torch.zeros(tgt_len, past_key_values_length, dtype=dtype, device=device), mask], dim=-1) return mask[None, None, :, :].expand(bsz, 1, tgt_len, tgt_len + past_key_values_length) ``` So `_make_causal_mask` returns `(bsz, 1, tgt_len, tgt_len + past_key_values_length)`. We call `.contiguous()` after permute, which matches `_shape` that calls `.contiguous()`. So that should match. But since we call `.contiguous()` after `.permute()`, it matches `.transpose(1,2).contiguous()`. Wait, `_shape` does: ```python def _shape(self, tensor, seq_len, bsz): return tensor.view(bsz, seq_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2).contiguous() ``` So it's `view` then `transpose(1,2)` then `contiguous()`. Our C++ code does `view(...).permute({0,2,1,3}).contiguous()`. For a 4D tensor, `transpose(1,2)` is equivalent to `permute({0,2,1,3})`. Yes. Actually, `key_states` come from the same `hidden_states`, so they have the same seq length (`tgt_len`). But `_shape` uses `seq_len` for the view. If `seq_len=-1`, it means infer from the tensor. So our code uses `seq_len` for both, which matches. `value_states = self._shape(self.v_proj(hidden_states), -1, bsz)`. `attn_weights = torch.bmm(query_states, key_states.transpose(1, 2))`. `query_states` is `(B*H, T, D)`, `key_states.transpose` is `(B*H, D, T)`. Good. Softmax dim=-1. `attn_output = torch.bmm(attn_weights, value_states)`. `value_states` is `(B*H, T, D)`. Output `(B*H, T, D)`. So overall, our C++ code should produce numerically identical outputs (to within floating point differences from different operation ordering). But note: `at::layer_norm` might use a different algorithm or order than `nn.LayerNorm` in Python, but both call the same ATen implementation under the hood. `nn.LayerNorm` in Python dispatches to `F.layer_norm` which calls `at::layer_norm`. Let's structure the Python file. ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoConfig # C++ source cpp_sources = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <vector> #include <cmath> torch::Tensor bart_forward(torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor attention_mask, std::vector<torch::Tensor> w, int has_layernorm_embedding, int has_final_layer_norm) { int idx = 0; During decoding, there is also attention_mask for padding but since batch_size=1 and all tokens are nonzero, there is no padding mask. In reference computation, the attention mask added to attention scores is `(1.0 - expanded_mask) * torch.finfo(dtype).min` where expanded_mask is triangular. Since there is no padding, the result is the standard causal mask. Good. However, in the reference model, `attention_mask` is not passed in forward (input_ids only). Inside `BartDecoder`, it builds causal mask itself: `causal_mask = self._prepare_decoder_attention_mask(attention_mask, input_shape, inputs_embeds, past_key_values_length)`. Since `attention_mask=None`, it creates a causal mask of shape `(batch, 1, seq_len, seq_len)` with `torch.finfo.min` on upper triangle. That matches our mask. 4. The `self_attn` in Bart has `embed_dim, num_heads, dropout, bias=True`. The q/k/v/out_proj are linear. In the reference code, `q * self.scaling` where `self.scaling = self.head_dim**-0.5`. Apply after q projection. In some BART implementations, the scaling is applied after q projection before reshaping. But in the huggingface implementation, scaled_dot_product_attention multiplies q by scale before matmul or passes scale to kernel. Regardless, mathematically equivalent if scale is applied to q (because q @ k^T scaled by 1/sqrt(head_dim)). If we apply scale to q before bmm(q,k^T), then compute softmax, it's equivalent. Yes because: scale*(q @ k^T) = (scale*q) @ k^T = q @ (scale*k^T), and softmax is invariant under scaling? Wait softmax is not invariant under scaling of the logits. In self-attention, scaling is applied before softmax; dividing by sqrt(d) reduces variance. So we need to scale q (or the product) before softmax. In reference, the `q` is multiplied by scaling before matmul with k (or `torch.matmul(q, k.transpose(-2, -1)) * scaling`). So scaling `q` by scale works because `matmul(s*q, k^T) = s * matmul(q, k^T)`. Good. 5. Wait `at::embedding(weight, indices, padding_idx, scale_grad_by_freq, sparse)`? Actually C++ `at::embedding` signature: `at::Tensor embedding(const Tensor & weight, const Tensor & indices, int64_t padding_idx=-1, bool scale_grad_by_freq=false, bool sparse=false)`. So passing `1` as padding_idx replicates the `padding_idx=1` behavior: the vector at index 1 will be zeroed. `embed_positions` also has `padding_idx=1`; but position indices are never 1. However the actual embedding module has `padding_idx` set? Wait, in `modeling_bart.py`: ```python class BartLearnedPositionalEmbedding(nn.Embedding): def __init__(self, num_embeddings: int, embedding_dim: int, padding_idx: int): # Bart is set up so that if padding_idx is specified then offset = 2 self.offset = 2 super().__init__(num_embeddings + self.offset, embedding_dim, padding_idx=padding_idx) ``` So the embedding weight has `padding_idx=1`. But the positions are always >= 2, so the `padding_idx` has no effect. Setting `at::embedding(embed_positions_weight, positions, 1)` or `1` is okay, or `padding_idx=-100` works because it never triggers. Actually in BART, the pad token id is 1. The input_ids from `get_inputs()` are integers from [0, vocab_size). There is a chance some tokens equal 1, so we need to zero out that embedding. I should change padding_idx to 1 for embed_tokens. For embed_positions, we can pass 1 as well to match module. 10. One additional subtlety: In Bart, after embedding, the hidden states are the sum of token embeddings and position embeddings. Then there is `layernorm_embedding`. Then dropout. Our implementation omits dropout as expected (eval mode, reference likely also in eval or dropout disabled). Good. 11. The attention causal mask in Bart is not just triangular; it's a 4D mask of shape [batch, 1, seq_len, seq_len], where the elements are `0` or a large negative number. In our C++ code, we compute `attn_weights = attn_weights.view({batch, num_heads, seq_len, seq_len})`, add `attention_mask` (which has shape [1,1,seq_len,seq_len]), then view back. This is broadcasted correctly. Need to make sure `attention_mask` is `torch.finfo(dtype).min` on the upper triangle. The reference mask: in `BartDecoder`, `_prepare_decoder_attention_mask` returns a 4D mask of shape [batch, 1, tgt_len, tgt_len]; values are `0.0` where allowed, `torch.finfo.min` where disallowed. That is what we pass. But the reference model (forward) returns logits of shape [batch, seq_len, vocab_size]. We just compute that. 12. No `attention_mask`. Therefore `BartDecoder.forward` computes `combined_attention_mask = _make_causal_mask(...)`. Since there is no attention_mask input, `expanded_attn_mask` is None. So combined_attention_mask is just the causal mask. The `_make_causal_mask` function: ```python def _make_causal_mask(input_ids_shape: torch.Size, dtype: torch.dtype, past_key_values_length: int = 0): bsz, tgt_len = input_ids_shape mask = torch.full((tgt_len, tgt_len), torch.finfo(dtype).min) mask_cond = torch.arange(mask.size(-1)) mask.masked_fill_(mask_cond < (mask_cond + 1).view(mask.size(-1), 1), 0) mask = mask.to(dtype) if past_key_values_length > 0: mask = torch.cat([torch.zeros(tgt_len, past_key_values_length, dtype=dtype), mask], dim=-1) return mask[None, None, :, :].expand(bsz, 1, tgt_len, tgt_len + past_key_values_length) ``` So the causal mask shape is `(1, 1, tgt_len, tgt_len)` expanded to `(bsz, 1, tgt_len, tgt_len)`. In our case bsz=1. In `BartAttention.forward`, the attention mask is added to the attn_weights (after scaling). The attn_weights shape is `(bsz * num_heads, tgt_len, tgt_len)`. The attention_mask is reshaped to `(bsz, 1, tgt_len, tgt_len)` and broadcast. In our C++ code, we added `attention_mask` of shape `(1, 1, seq_len, seq_len)` to `attn_weights` of shape `(batch, num_heads, seq_len, seq_len)`. This seems correct. The evaluation harness will likely call `model.eval()` before benchmarking. But for correctness tests, does it eval? Usually yes, it sets eval mode. However, dropout with p=0.1 still could produce differences if not exactly zero? In eval mode, dropout modules return input unchanged. So we can ignore dropout in our custom kernel. Good. Now, the `BartAttention` implementation details: ```python class BartAttention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, embed_dim, num_heads, dropout=0.0, is_decoder=False, bias=True): ... self.scaling = self.head_dim ** -0.5 self.q_proj = nn.Linear(embed_dim, embed_dim, bias=bias) self.k_proj = nn.Linear(embed_dim, embed_dim, bias=bias) self.v_proj = nn.Linear(embed_dim, embed_dim, bias=bias) self.out_proj = nn.Linear(embed_dim, embed_dim, bias=bias) ... ``` In forward: ```python def forward(self, hidden_states, key_value_states=None, past_key_value=None, attention_mask=None, layer_head_mask=None, output_attentions=False): bsz, tgt_len, _ = hidden_states.size() query_states = self.q_proj(hidden_states) * self.scaling ... if key_value_states is None: key_states = self._shape(self.k_proj(hidden_states), -1, bsz) value_states = self._shape(self.v_proj(hidden_states), -1, bsz) else: ... proj_shape = (bsz * self.num_heads, -1, self.head_dim) query_states = self._shape(query_states, tgt_len, bsz).view(*proj_shape) key_states = key_states.view(*proj_shape) value_states = value_states.view(*proj_shape) src_len = key_states.size(1) attn_weights = torch.bmm(query_states, key_states.transpose(1, 2)) if attn_weights.size() != (bsz * self.num_heads, tgt_len, src_len): ... if attention_mask is not None: attn_weights = attn_weights.view(bsz, self.num_heads, tgt_len, src_len) + attention_mask attn_weights = attn_weights.view(bsz * self.num_heads, tgt_len, src_len) attn_weights = nn.functional.softmax(attn_weights, dim=-1) if output_attentions: ... attn_probs = nn.functional.dropout(attn_weights, p=self.dropout, training=self.training) attn_output = torch.bmm(attn_probs, value_states) ... attn_output = attn_output.view(bsz, self.num_heads, tgt_len, self.head_dim) attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1, 2) attn_output = attn_output.reshape(bsz, tgt_len, self.embed_dim) attn_output = self.out_proj(attn_output) attn_output = nn.functional.dropout(attn_output, p=self.dropout, training=self.training) return attn_output, attn_weights, past_key_value ``` Note: `self._shape` does: ```python def _shape(self, tensor, seq_len, bsz): return tensor.view(bsz, seq_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2).contiguous() ``` So query_states: `self.q_proj(hidden_states) * self.scaling`. Then `_shape(query_states, tgt_len, bsz)` which transposes dims 1 and 2. Then view to `(bsz * num_heads, tgt_len, head_dim)`. This matches our C++ code, except our C++ code uses `view({batch, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous().view({batch * num_heads, seq_len, head_dim})`. That is equivalent. Good. Now, the reference does: ```python attn_weights = nn.functional.softmax(attn_weights, dim=-1) ``` which is the same as `at::softmax(attn_weights, -1)`. Then dropout (skipped in eval). Then `attn_output = torch.bmm(attn_probs, value_states)`. Then: ```python attn_output = attn_output.view(bsz, self.num_heads, tgt_len, self.head_dim) attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1, 2) attn_output = attn_output.reshape(bsz, tgt_len, self.embed_dim) ``` Wait, `transpose(1, 2)` then `reshape`. The transpose does not make it contiguous, but `reshape` can handle it if the strides are compatible. In our code, we do: ```cpp attn_output = attn_output.view({batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim}) .permute({0, 2, 1, 3}) .contiguous() .view({batch, seq_len, d_model}); ``` That should produce the same result (after out_proj). Actually, the reference does `transpose(1,2)` which gives shape `(bsz, tgt_len, num_heads, head_dim)`. Wait: `attn_output` starts as `(bsz, num_heads, tgt_len, head_dim)` after view. `transpose(1,2)` gives `(bsz, tgt_len, num_heads, head_dim)`. Then `reshape` to `(bsz, tgt_len, embed_dim)`. In older transformers, `gelu_python` might use `0.5 * x * (1 + tanh(...))` (approximate). However, `ACT2FN["gelu"]` in recent transformers maps to `nn.GELU()` with `approximate="none"`.
